Key Rule: Building window patterns reflect architectural design. Modern buildings tend to be constant; older buildings show decreasing or irregular patterns. Identifying the type of sequence is the key skill.

Set C Design and Create

Design a Rangoli pattern with 4-fold symmetry. Describe the sequence of shapes from the centre outwards (e.g., 1 dot, 4 petals, 8 dots, 12 leaves…).
Medium
Answer

Answer: Centre=1, Ring 1=4, Ring 2=8, Ring 3=12 — arithmetic from Ring 1 (d=4), total=25

Method: Design a pattern with 4-fold symmetry, describe the sequence
Step 1: 4-fold symmetry means the pattern looks the same when rotated by 90°. Each ring adds shapes in groups of 4.
Step 2: Design: Centre = 1 dot. Ring 1 = 4 petals. Ring 2 = 8 dots. Ring 3 = 12 leaves. The number of elements per ring: 4, 8, 12 — arithmetic with d=4.
Step 3: Total elements = 1 + 4 + 8 + 12 = 25. Note: 25 = 5² — perfect square!
Step 4: Formula for Ring n (n ≥ 1): Ring(n) = 4n. Total through Ring n = 1 + 4(1+2+…+n) = 1 + 4×n(n+1)/2 = 1 + 2n(n+1) = 2n²+2n+1.
Step 5: Verify: n=3: 2(9)+2(3)+1 = 18+6+1 = 25 ✓.
Key Rule: Symmetric patterns with k-fold symmetry grow in multiples of k. 4-fold symmetry → rings of 4, 8, 12, 16… (arithmetic with d=4).
Create a savings plan for buying a ₹3 600 bicycle in 12 weeks. Design an arithmetic savings sequence where you save the same amount per week. How much per week?
Easy
Answer

Answer: Save ₹300 per week; Sequence: 300, 600, 900, …, 3600

Method: Divide total by weeks, build arithmetic sequence
Step 1: Total needed = ₹3 600. Number of weeks = 12. Weekly savings = ₹3 600 ÷ 12 = ₹300 per week.
Step 2: Cumulative savings sequence: After Week 1=₹300, Week 2=₹600, Week 3=₹900, …, Week 12=₹3 600.
Step 3: This is an arithmetic sequence with a₁=300 and d=300. Formula: Tₙ = 300n.
Step 4: Verify: T₁=300 ✓, T₁₂=300×12=3600 ✓. The bicycle is fully paid for after week 12.
Key Rule: To save a fixed amount evenly over n periods: weekly saving = total ÷ n. The cumulative savings form an arithmetic sequence with d = weekly saving.
Design a training schedule for a 5 km run: Week 1=1 km, Week 2=1.5 km, Week 3=2 km… How many weeks to reach 5 km? What is the sequence type?
Easy
Answer

Answer: 9 weeks to reach 5 km; Arithmetic sequence (d=0.5 km)

Method: Build formula, solve for the target term
Step 1: Week 1=1 km, Week 2=1.5 km, Week 3=2 km. d = 0.5 km/week. a₁ = 1 km.
Step 2: Formula: Tₙ = 1 + (n−1)×0.5 = 0.5 + 0.5n.
Step 3: Verify: T₁=0.5+0.5=1 ✓, T₃=0.5+1.5=2 ✓.
Step 4: Solve Tₙ = 5: 0.5 + 0.5n = 5 → 0.5n = 4.5 → n = 9.
Step 5: Week 9: T₉ = 0.5 + 4.5 = 5 km ✓. By Week 9 the runner reaches the 5 km goal. This is a realistic couch-to-5K style training plan!
Key Rule: Athletic training plans are often arithmetic sequences — a constant increase per week builds fitness gradually and safely.
Plan a school garden with 5 rows of flower beds. Row 1 has 3 beds, each row has 2 more. Design the layout, write the sequence, and find the total number of beds.
Medium
Answer

Answer: Sequence 3, 5, 7, 9, 11; Total = 35 beds

Method: Design arithmetic layout, sum the sequence
Step 1: Row 1=3, Row 2=5, Row 3=7, Row 4=9, Row 5=11. Common difference d=2 (two more beds per row).
Step 2: Sequence: 3, 5, 7, 9, 11. First term a₁=3, last term a₅=11, n=5 rows.
Step 3: Total beds = S₅ = (n/2)(a₁+aₙ) = (5/2)(3+11) = (5/2)×14 = 5×7 = 35 beds.
Step 4: Verify: 3+5=8, 8+7=15, 15+9=24, 24+11=35 ✓.
Step 5: The garden grows wider each row, which is typical for triangle-shaped or fan-shaped garden designs.
Key Rule: Sum of arithmetic sequence: Sₙ = (n/2)(first + last). Always verify by adding up terms individually.
Design a Fibonacci-inspired spiral bookmark. Mark squares of side 1,1,2,3,5,8 cm on paper. Calculate the total area and the ratio of the long side to the short side.
Medium
Answer

Answer: Total area = 104 cm²; bookmark is 8×13 cm; ratio = 1.625 ≈ φ

Method: Compute areas, find total, calculate ratio
Step 1: Fibonacci squares: sides 1, 1, 2, 3, 5, 8 cm. Areas: 1, 1, 4, 9, 25, 64 cm².
Step 2: Total area = 1+1+4+9+25+64 = 104 cm².
Step 3: When Fibonacci squares are arranged as in the Fibonacci spiral, they form a rectangle. The overall rectangle = 8 cm × 13 cm (next two Fibonacci numbers). Verify: 8×13 = 104 cm² ✓.
Step 4: Ratio of long to short side = 13/8 = 1.625 ≈ φ = 1.618…. The bookmark is very close to the golden rectangle!
Step 5: Each time you add the next Fibonacci square, the rectangle's aspect ratio gets closer to φ.
Key Rule: Fibonacci squares tile into a rectangle whose aspect ratio converges to φ (the golden ratio). This is why Fibonacci spirals appear golden!

Design a "pyramid donation" scheme for a school charity: Student A donates ₹1, asks 2 friends to donate ₹1 each (they each ask 2 friends). After 5 rounds, how many students donate? What is the total collected?
Hard
Answer

Answer: 31 students donate; total collected = ₹31

Method: Geometric sequence for pyramid donation scheme
Step 1: Round 1: 1 student donates. Round 2: 2 students. Round 3: 4. Round 4: 8. Round 5: 16. Sequence: 1, 2, 4, 8, 16 — geometric with r=2.
Step 2: Formula for Round n: Tₙ = 2ⁿ⁻¹. Verify: T₁=1✓, T₅=16✓.
Step 3: Total students = 1+2+4+8+16 = sum of geometric series = (2⁵−1)/(2−1) = 31.
Step 4: Total collected = 31 × ₹1 = ₹31. Each person donates ₹1.
Step 5: If this were a pyramid scheme requiring each person to donate ₹100, total = ₹3 100 in just 5 rounds. By Round 10 it needs 1023 people — exponential growth is powerful (and potentially dangerous in financial schemes)!
Key Rule: Geometric doubling grows very fast. Sum of geometric series: S = a(rⁿ−1)/(r−1). For r=2: S = 2ⁿ − 1.
Create a "temperature prediction" model: if today = 28°C and the forecast says −1°C per day for 6 days, design the sequence. On which day does it drop below 23°C?
Hard
Answer

Answer: Tₙ = 28−n; temperature drops below 23°C on Day 6

Method: Decreasing arithmetic sequence, solve inequality
Step 1: Day 0 = 28°C. Decrease = 1°C per day (d = −1). Sequence: 28, 27, 26, 25, 24, 23, 22, …
Step 2: Formula: Tₙ = 28 − n (where n = days from today). Verify: T₀=28 ✓, T₁=27 ✓, T₆=22 ✓.
Step 3: Solve Tₙ < 23: 28 − n < 23 → −n < −5 → n > 5. So n ≥ 6.
Step 4: T₆ = 28 − 6 = 22°C < 23°C ✓. T₅ = 23°C (not below 23). Day 6 is the first day below 23°C.
Key Rule: "First day below X" → solve Tₙ < X, round n up. Check both n and n−1.

Answer

Set D Investigate and Present

Investigate: measure the arm span and height of 10 people. Is the ratio arm span:height close to 1:1? Is this a pattern in humans? (Da Vinci's Vitruvian Man suggests yes.)
Hard
Answer

Answer: Arm span ≈ height; ratio ≈ 1:1 for most people (da Vinci's insight)

Method: Collect measurements, calculate ratios, compare to known pattern
Step 1: Measure arm span and height for 10 people. Sample data (cm): Person 1: span=165, height=166. Person 2: 178/179. Person 3: 172/171. Person 4: 161/162. Person 5: 185/184.
Step 2: Calculate ratios span:height. All ≈ 1.000 (ranging from 0.994 to 1.006).
Step 3: Mean ratio ≈ 1.00. This confirms arm span ≈ height — a 1:1 ratio.
Step 4: Da Vinci's Vitruvian Man (1490) depicted this proportion explicitly: "a man's arm span equals his height." This is one of the most famous patterns in human anatomy. It holds for adults and most people within ±2%.
Step 5: Why does this pattern exist? It reflects the symmetrical growth of the human skeleton — all limbs grow proportionally.
Key Rule: Human body proportions form consistent ratios (arm span:height ≈ 1:1). Statistical analysis of measurements confirms this mathematical pattern in biology.
Music rhythm: a 4/4 time signature has beats: 1,2,3,4,1,2,3,4… A 3/4 waltz: 1,2,3,1,2,3… Find the LCM of their bar lengths. After how many beats do both rhythms start a new bar simultaneously?
Hard
Answer

Answer: LCM(4,3) = 12 — both rhythms align every 12 beats

Method: Find the LCM of two repeating pattern lengths
Step 1: 4/4 time: bars of 4 beats. New bar starts at beats: 1, 5, 9, 13, 17, … (arithmetic sequence, d=4).
Step 2: 3/4 time (waltz): bars of 3 beats. New bar starts at beats: 1, 4, 7, 10, 13, … (arithmetic sequence, d=3).
Step 3: When do both start a new bar simultaneously? Find the LCM of 4 and 3. LCM(4,3) = 12 (since 4=2² and 3=3¹ are coprime, LCM=4×3=12).
Step 4: After 12 beats, both rhythms start a new bar at the same time. The 4/4 has completed 3 bars; the 3/4 has completed 4 bars. ✓
Step 5: In performance, this alignment moment creates a natural "downbeat" in music played in both time signatures simultaneously.
Key Rule: Two repeating patterns with periods a and b synchronise every LCM(a,b) steps. This is why LCM is musically important!
Population growth: a city has 500 000 people. It grows at 2% per year. Is this arithmetic or geometric? Write the population for 5 years and estimate when it doubles.
Hard
Answer

Answer: Geometric (ratio=1.02); population doubles in ≈36 years (Rule of 72)

Method: Build geometric sequence, apply Rule of 72
Step 1: 2% annual growth means each year the population is multiplied by 1.02. This is a geometric sequence with r = 1.02.
Step 2: Year-by-year population:
Year 1: 500 000. Year 2: 500 000 × 1.02 = 510 000. Year 3: 510 000 × 1.02 = 520 200. Year 4: 530 604. Year 5: 541 216. Year 6: 552 040.
Step 3: Formula: Pₙ = 500 000 × (1.02)ⁿ⁻¹.
Step 4: Doubling time using Rule of 72: divide 72 by the annual growth rate percentage: 72 ÷ 2 = 36 years. So population doubles (from 500 000 to 1 000 000) in approximately 36 years.
Step 5: Compare arithmetic vs geometric: arithmetic growth (+2% of original = +10 000/year) would take 50 years to double; geometric growth is faster because interest compounds.
Key Rule: Percentage growth is always geometric (ratio = 1 + rate). Rule of 72: doubling time ≈ 72 / (rate%). Arithmetic sequences cannot model compound growth.
Investigate the "handshake problem" in your class. If n=30 students each shake every other student's hand once, total handshakes = n(n−1)/2. Calculate for your class. How many handshakes if the teacher also joins?
Hard
Answer

Answer: 30 students → 435 handshakes; 31 (with teacher) → 465 handshakes

Method: Apply handshake formula H(n) = n(n−1)/2
Step 1: Formula: H(n) = n(n−1)/2. For n=30 students: H(30) = 30×29/2 = 870/2 = 435.
Step 2: With teacher added (n=31): H(31) = 31×30/2 = 930/2 = 465.
Step 3: Additional handshakes from teacher = 465 − 435 = 30. The teacher shakes hands with all 30 students — exactly 30 new handshakes. ✓
Step 4: In general, adding 1 person to a group of n adds exactly n handshakes: H(n+1) − H(n) = n.
Step 5: Real-world scale: at a business conference with 100 people: H(100) = 100×99/2 = 4950 handshakes!
Key Rule: Handshake formula H(n) = n(n−1)/2. Adding one person to a group of n adds exactly n handshakes. This is both a combinatorics formula and the (n−1)th triangular number.

Architecture: the Eiffel Tower is 330 m tall. Its 4 legs form a square. Measure a model or drawing and find if its height:base ratio is close to the golden ratio φ. Research and report.
Hard
Answer

Answer: Height:base ratio ≈ 2.64 (not φ), but Eiffel Tower uses recursive mathematical proportions

Method: Calculate ratio, compare to φ, research the actual design
Step 1: Eiffel Tower: height = 330 m (including antenna). Base = 125 m wide.
Step 2: Height:base ratio = 330:125 = 2.64. Compare to φ = 1.618. Not equal — 2.64 ÷ 1.618 ≈ 1.63 ≈ φ again? No, 2.64 ≠ φ and is not a simple Fibonacci ratio.

Investigate Pascal's triangle. Write 8 rows. Find: (a) the row sums, (b) the triangular numbers, (c) the Fibonacci numbers. Where are they hidden?
Hard
Answer

Answer: Pascal's triangle contains powers of 2, triangular numbers, and Fibonacci numbers!

Method: Investigate three hidden patterns in Pascal's triangle
Step 1: Write 8 rows (Row 0 to Row 7):
Row 0: 1. Row 1: 1 1. Row 2: 1 2 1. Row 3: 1 3 3 1. Row 4: 1 4 6 4 1. Row 5: 1 5 10 10 5 1. Row 6: 1 6 15 20 15 6 1. Row 7: 1 7 21 35 35 21 7 1.
Step 2: (a) Row sums: 1, 2, 4, 8, 16, 32, 64, 128 = 2⁰, 2¹, 2², …, 2⁷. Powers of 2!
Step 3: (b) Triangular numbers: Found on the 3rd diagonal (from left, starting at 1): 1, 3, 6, 10, 15, 21, 28. These are T₁ through T₇!
Step 4: (c) Fibonacci numbers: Sum the "shallow diagonals" going up-right: 1 / 1 / 1+1=2 / 1+2=3 / 1+3+1=5 / 1+4+3=8 / 1+5+6+1=13 / … → 1, 1, 2, 3, 5, 8, 13, 21 — Fibonacci! ✓
Step 5: Pascal's triangle also contains square numbers, powers of 11, hockey stick patterns, Catalan numbers, and more — it is one of the richest structures in mathematics.
Key Rule: Pascal's triangle is a mathematical treasure chest — row sums = powers of 2, diagonal = triangular numbers, shallow diagonal sums = Fibonacci. One table, infinite patterns!
The "Monty Hall problem" involves pattern thinking: 3 doors, 1 prize. You pick door 1. Host opens a losing door. Should you switch? Simulate 30 trials by recording outcomes and track the win rate when switching vs staying.
Hard
Answer

Answer: Switching wins ≈ 2/3 of the time; always switch! The pattern emerges from probability.

Method: Simulate trials, record outcomes, analyse win rate pattern
Step 1: Set up: 3 doors (A, B, C), prize behind one. You pick a door. Host opens a LOSING door (never the prize door). You decide: stay or switch?
Step 2: Mathematical analysis: If you STAY — you only win if you initially picked the prize door (probability 1/3). If you SWITCH — you win if you initially picked a LOSING door (probability 2/3).
Step 3: Simulate 30 trials. Expected results: Switch → win ≈ 20 times (2/3 × 30). Stay → win ≈ 10 times (1/3 × 30).
Step 4: After 30 trials, the win rates will approximate 2/3 (switch) vs 1/3 (stay). This is the Monty Hall Problem — one of the most famous counterintuitive results in probability.
Step 5: The "pattern" here is probabilistic: switching always gives double the winning probability. This is a mathematical law, not a coincidence.
Key Rule: The Monty Hall problem: always switch! Switching wins with probability 2/3. Mathematics can reveal patterns that contradict our intuition — simulation confirms the theory.
Final project: Choose any Chapter 1 topic (patterns 1–12). Design a 10-question quiz for a younger student using only real-world examples from your neighbourhood or school. Include an answer key and explain the pattern concept being tested.
Hard
Answer

Answer: Open-ended project — assessment criteria provided; model example below

Method: Design a 10-question quiz using real-world patterns, with answer key
Sample Quiz Topic: "Patterns in Our School Canteen"
Q1 (Easy): The canteen makes 50 samosas on Monday, 60 on Tuesday, 70 on Wednesday. What type of sequence? How many on Friday?
Answer key: Arithmetic, d=10. Friday = T₅ = 90 samosas.
Q2 (Easy): Juice price: ₹10 for 1 glass, ₹20 for 2, ₹30 for 3. What is the nth term formula?
Answer key: Tₙ = 10n.
Q3–Q10: Progressively harder questions on: geometric growth of waste, Fibonacci portions, nth term for weekly meal count, etc.
Assessment criteria:
(1) Real-world context is genuine and local to the student's school/neighbourhood.
(2) Questions progress from easy (identify type) to hard (find nth term, solve for n).
(3) Pattern rule is clearly stated in words AND as a formula.
(4) Answer key includes working, not just the final answer.
(5) Language is simple enough for a student 2 years younger.
Key Rule: Teaching mathematics to others is the deepest form of understanding. Creating a quiz forces you to think about what makes a pattern, how to explain it, and how to challenge someone else — the pinnacle of Pattern Mastery!
